    </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are now considered as a low-cost alternative to lithium-ion technologies for large-scale energy storage applications; however, their safety is still a matter of great concern for practical applications.  In this paper, a safer sodium-ion battery is proposed by introducing a nonflammable phosphate electrolyte (trimethyl phosphate, TMP) coupled with NaNi0.35Mn0.35Fe0.3O2 cathode and Sb-based alloy anode.  The physical and electrochemical compatibilities of the TMP electrolyte are investigated by igniting, ionic conductivity, cyclic voltammetry, and charge-discharge measurements.  The results exhibit that the TMP electrolyte with FEC additive is completely nonflammable and has wide electrochemical window (0-4.5 V vs.  Na/Na(+)), in which both the Sb-based anode and NaNi0.35Mn0.35Fe0.3O2 cathode show high reversible capacity and cycling stability, similarly as in carbonate electrolyte.  Based on these results, a nonflammable sodium-ion battery is constructed by use of Sb anode, NaNi0.35Mn0.35Fe0.3O2 cathode, and TMP + 10 vol% FEC electrolyte, which works very well with considerable capacity and cyclability, demonstrating a promising prospect to build safer sodium-ion batteries for large-scale energy storage applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcTKk00v_YolRQqdek-gHSrIfW6udTcc2ebbKwD8xPR6F7ntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC2svnvVWrtg%253D%253D&amp;md5=d85cad700e01cb17d1a849ea41354791</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2007.10.084"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Feng, J. The optimized Na4Mn9O18 display high crystallinity and a homogeneous nanowire structure this provides a mech.-stable structure as well as a short diffusion path for Na-ion intercalation and extn.  The Na4Mn9O18 nanowires have a high reversible capacity of 128 mA-h/g at 0.1 C, excellent cycleability (77% capacity retention for 1000 cycles at 0.5 C), and promising rate capability for Na-ion battery applications.  The outstanding performance of the Na4Mn9O18 nanowires makes them a promising candidate to construct a viable and low-cost Na-ion battery system for upcoming power and energy storage systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrES3043e-CdrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljeYhrv_yhG_Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXntVGrtbg%253D&amp;md5=015afcbec81da24c355d3768c299be72</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201401946"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yuan, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Feng, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Honeycomb-Layered Na<sub>3</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub>: A High-Rate and Cycle-Stable Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Suppressing the P2-O2 Phase Transition of Na0.67Mn0.67Ni0.33O2 by Magnesium Substitution for Improved Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Peng-Fei; You, Ya; Yin, Ya-Xia; Wang, Yue-Sheng; Wan, Li-Jun; Gu, Lin; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">55</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temp. Na-ion batteries (SIBs) showed great promise in grid-scale energy storage, portable electronics, and elec. vehicles because of the abundance of low-cost Na.  Na-based layered oxides with a P2-type layered framework have been considered as one of the most promising cathode materials for SIBs.  However, they suffer from the undesired P2-O2 phase transition, which leads to rapid capacity decay and limited reversible capacities.  Herein, this problem can be significantly mitigated by substituting some of the Ni ions with Mg to obtain Na0.67Mn0.67Ni0.33-xMgxO2 (0â¤xâ¤0.33).  Both the reversible capacity and the capacity retention of the P2-type cathode material were remarkably improved as the P2-O2 phase transition was thus suppressed during cycling.  This strategy might also be applicable to the modulation of the phys. and chem. properties of layered oxides and provides new insight into the rational design of high-capacity and highly stable cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqs1w8P4jHWorVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljeYhrv_yhG_Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XntFCmtLo%253D&amp;md5=db98750372bffe253f4c4d51e3f452cf</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1039/C6CC09378G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, P.-F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yao, H.-R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zuo, T.-T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yin, Y.-X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, Y.-G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Novel P2-Type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Mg<sub>1/6</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.                         cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Novel P2-type Na2/3Ni1/6Mg1/6Ti2/3O2 as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Peng-Fei; Yao, Hu-Rong; Zuo, Tong-Tong; Yin, Ya-Xia; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Communications (Cambridge, United Kingdom)</span>

                        cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Phosphate Framework Electrode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fang Yongjin; Zhang Jiexin; Ai Xinping; Cao Yuliang; Yang Hanxi; Xiao Lifen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as a promising alternative for the next generation of electric storage systems due to their similar electrochemistry to Li-ion batteries and the low cost of sodium resources.  Exploring appropriate electrode materials with decent electrochemical performance is the key issue for development of sodium ion batteries.  Due to the high structural stability, facile reaction mechanism and rich structural diversity, phosphate framework materials have attracted increasing attention as promising electrode materials for sodium ion batteries.  Herein, we review the latest advances and progresses in the exploration of phosphate framework materials especially related to single-phosphates, pyrophosphates and mixed-phosphates.  We provide the detailed and comprehensive understanding of structure-composition-performance relationship of materials and try to show the advantages and disadvantages of the materials for use in SIBs.  In addition, some new perspectives about phosphate framework materials for SIBs are also discussed.  Phosphate framework materials will be a competitive and attractive choice for use as electrodes in the next-generation of energy storage devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcTMcbcHlPuAMBwLZxei1HN8fW6udTcc2eYrguhBXIIJ57ntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1cnhsFehsQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=5afdb3cd74a61df70f70c83d0ead6440</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit36" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b12620"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wei, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Highly Crystallized Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Potential applications of sodium-ion batteries in grid-scale energy storage, portable electronics and elec. vehicles have revitalized research interest in these batteries.  However, the performance of sodium-ion electrode materials has not been competitive with that of lithium-ion electrode materials.  Here we present sodium manganese hexacyanomanganate (Na2MnII[MnII(CN)6]), an open-framework crystal structure material, as a viable pos. electrode for sodium-ion batteries.  We demonstrate a high discharge capacity of 209 mAh g-1 at C/5 (40 mA g-1) and excellent capacity retention at high rates in a propylene carbonate electrolyte.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a top alternative to Li-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale renewable energy storage units due to their low cost and the abundance of Na-bearing precursors in the earth's mineral deposits.                         cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tin Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries Using Natural Wood Fiber as a Mechanical Buffer and Electrolyte Reservoir</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Hongli; Jia, Zheng; Chen, Yuchen; Weadock, Nicholas; Wan, Jiayu; Vaaland, Oeyvind; Han, Xiaogang; Li, Teng; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries offer an attractive option for low cost grid scale storage due to the abundance of Na.  Sn is touted as a high capacity anode for Na-ion batteries with a high theor. capacity of 847 mA-h/g, but it has several limitations such as large vol. expansion with cycling, slow kinetics, and unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation.  An anode consisting of a Sn thin film deposited on a hierarchical wood fiber substrate simultaneously addresses all the challenges assocd. with Sn anodes.  The soft nature of wood fibers effectively releases the mech. stresses assocd. with the sodiation process, and the mesoporous structure functions as an electrolyte reservoir that allows for ion transport through the outer and inner surface of the fiber.  These properties are confirmed exptl. and computationally.  A stable cycling performance of 400 cycles with an initial capacity of 339 mA-h/g is demonstrated; a significant improvement over other reported Sn nanostructures.  The soft and mesoporous wood fiber substrate can be used as a new platform for low cost Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrPjnqhdJ5q7rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhrl9uft2z0ew"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXosVegtr0%253D&amp;md5=b49c25ccb079701ddb3c8a22d15156ad</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1021/nl3016957"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sushko, M. The hollow carbon nanowires used as anode material for Na-ion batteries deliver a high reversible capacity of 251 mA-h/g and 82.2% capacity retention over 400 charge-discharge cycles between 1.2 and 0.01 V (vs. Na+/Na) at a const. current of 50 mA/g (0.2C).  Excellent cycling stability is also obsd. at an even higher charge-discharge rate.  A high reversible capacity of 149 mA-h/g also can be obtained at a current rate of 500 mA/g (2C).  The good Na-ion insertion property is attributed to the short diffusion distance in the hollow carbon nanowires and the large interlayer distance (0.37 nm) between the graphitic sheets, which agrees with the interlayered distance predicted by theor. calcns. to enable Na-ion insertion in carbon materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq9utH1EdajJbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhrl9uft2z0ew"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XotlShu78%253D&amp;md5=c54fcb809a340c023d9185f1d9f5e848</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2016.02.164"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jiang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanospherical-Like Manganese Monoxide/Reduced Graphene Oxide Composite Synthesized by Electron Beam Radiation as Anode Material for High-Performance Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Electrochim.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The first report on excellent cycling stability and superior rate capability of Na3V2(PO4)3 for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Saravanan, Kuppan; Mason, Chad W.; Rudola, Ashish; Wong, Kim Hai; Balaya, Palani</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries are attractive for the rapidly emerging large-scale energy storage market for intermittent renewable resources.  Currently a viable cathode material does not exist for practical non-aq. sodium ion battery applications.  Here we disclose a high performance, durable electrode material based on the 3D NASICON framework.  Porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C was synthesized using a novel soln.-based approach.  This material, as a cathode, is capable of delivering an energy storage capacity of â¼400 mWh/g vs. sodium metal.  Furthermore, at high current rates (10, 20 and 40 C), it displayed remarkable capacity retention.  Equally impressive is the long term cycle life.  Nearly 50% of the initial capacity was retained after 30,000 charge/discharge cycles at 40 C (4.7 A/g).  Notably, coulombic efficiency was 99.68% (av.) over the course of cycling.  To the best of our knowledge, the combination of high energy d., high power d. and ultra long cycle life demonstrated here has never been reported before for sodium ion batteries.  We believe our findings will have profound implications for developing large-scale energy storage systems for renewable energy sources.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqDJwVCxsh14rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhrl9uft2z0ew"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXmtFaks7Y%253D&amp;md5=de231bc6c1bc62101fab4472580ebdfa</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit46" data-doi="10.3866/PKU.WHXB201610111"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, Y.-J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, Z.-X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.-P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.-X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.-L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Developments in Cathode Materials for Na Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Acta Phys.-Chim. We report a facile spray-drying method to synthesize graphene-scaffolded Na3V2(PO4)3 microspheres (NVP@rGO), in which nanocryst. Na3V2(PO4)3 is embedded in graphene sheets to form porous microspheres.                         cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In search of an optimized electrolyte for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ponrouch, Alexandre; Marchante, Elena; Courty, Matthieu; Tarascon, Jean-Marie; Palacin, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrolytes are essential for the proper functioning of any battery technol. and the emerging Na-ion technol. is no exception.  Hence, a major focus on battery research is to identify the most appropriate formulation so as to minimize interface reactions and enhance both cell performances and safety aspects.  In order to identify suitable electrolyte formulations for Na-ion chem. we benchmarked various electrolytes contg. diverse solvent mixts. (cyclic, acyclic carbonates, glymes) and Na-based salts having either F-based or perchlorate anions and measured viscosity, ionic cond., and thermal and electrochem. stability.  The binary EC:PC solvent mixt. has emerged as the best solvent formulation and has been used to test the performance of Na/hard carbon cells with both NaClO4 and NaPF6 as dissolved salts.  Hard carbon electrodes having reversible capacities of 200 mA h g-1 with decent rate capability and excellent capacity retention (&gt;180 cycles) were demonstrated.  Moreover, DSC heating curves demonstrated that fully sodiated hard carbon cycled in NaPF6-EC:PC exhibits the highest exothermic onset temp. and nearly the lowest enthalpy of reaction, thus making this electrolyte most attractive for the development of Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp6iY9--X3CQLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljrMZiulgPpIQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38Xht1WrsLnE&amp;md5=4cc627e9703d3883d10ee8eccc42e514</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA04491J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Porous NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> Nanocubes: A High-Rate Nonaqueous Sodium Anode Material with More Than 10 000 Cycle Life</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Porous NaTi2(PO4)3 nanocubes: a high-rate nonaqueous sodium anode material with more than 10 000 cycle life</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Gongzheng; Song, Huawei; Wu, Mingmei; Wang, Chengxin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Besides targeting low-voltage intercalation, NaTi2(PO4)3 (NTP) is a promising neg. electrode material for non-aq. sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  However, the low electronic cond. of this material inhibits its potential applications.  Herein, we report the controllable synthesis of four interesting porous NTP nanocubes via a one-pot solvothermal method.  The as-synthesized products have shown excellent high-rate performance and cycling stability as SIB anodes.  After 10 000 cycles at a 10C rate, 75.5% of the initial capacity is retained, which exceeds those of most of the reported SIB anode materials.  Even at an extremely high rate of 100C, the NTP nanocubes can still deliver considerable reversible capacities after deep charging/discharging for 15 000 cycles.  The superior electrochem. performances can be attributed to their unique nanostructures.  We hope that such a finding of the new construction will enrich the NTP system and provide several possible candidates for SIB anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqcwgKt1_heH7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljrMZiulgPpIQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht1yqtbnN&amp;md5=7bb066b2f28f142e2bb451a501b20a7e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit54" data-doi="10.1007/s12274-016-1077-y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Roh, H.-K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, H.-K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, M.-S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, D.-H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chung, K.                         cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ synthesis of chemically bonded NaTi2(PO4)3/rGO 2D nanocomposite for high-rate sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Roh, Ha-Kyung; Kim, Hyun-Kyung; Kim, Myeong-Seong; Kim, Dong-Hyun; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Roh, Kwang Chul; Kim, Kwang-Bum</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1844-1855</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NRAEB5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1998-0000</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer GmbH</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A phase-pure NaTi2(PO4)3/reduced graphene oxide (rGO) nanocomposite was prepd. using a microwave-assisted one-pot method and subsequent heat treatment.  The well-crystd. NaTi2(PO4)3 nanoparticles (30-40 nm) were uniformly pptd. on rGO templates through Ti-O-C bonds.  The chem. interactions between the NaTi2(PO4)3 nanoparticles and rGO could immobilize the NaTi2(PO4)3 nanoparticles on the rGO sheets, which might be responsible for the excellent electrochem. performance of the nanocomposite.  The NaTi2(PO4)3/rGO nanocomposite exhibited a specific capacity of 128.6 mAÂ·hÂ·g-1 approaching the theor. value at a 0.1 C-rate with an excellent rate capability (72.9% capacity retention at 50 C-rate) and cycling performance (only 4.5% capacity loss after 1,000 cycles at a high rate of 10 C).  These properties were maintained even when the electrodes were prepd. without the use of an addnl. conducting agent.  The excellent sodium storage properties of the NaTi2(PO4)3/rGO nanocomposite could be attributed to the nano-sized NaTi2(PO4)3 particles, which significantly reduced the transport lengths for Na+ ions, and an intimate contact between the NaTi2(PO4)3 particles and rGO due to chem. bonding. [Figure not available: see fulltext.].</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGovusH6o6Sdg7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgAWeGB018mcw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XosFehsr8%253D&amp;md5=7c11a5f1b1efef7092b4edf7326203d3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit55" data-doi="10.1016/j.ijhydene.2015.12.090"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Vignarooban, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kushagra, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Elango, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Badami, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mellander, B. M.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Current Trends and Future Challenges of Electrolytes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Int. J. Hydrogen Energy</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">41</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">2829</span><span class="NLM_x">â</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">2846</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.ijhydene.2015.12.090</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D41%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D2829-2846%26journal%3DInt.%2BJ.%2BHydrogen%2BEnergy%26author%3DK.%2BVignarooban%26author%3DR.%2BKushagra%26author%3DA.%2BElango%26author%3DP.%2BBadami%26author%3DB.%2BE.%2BMellander%26author%3DX.%2BXu%26author%3DT.%2BG.%2BTucker%26author%3DC.%2BNam%26author%3DA.%2BM.%2BKannan%26title%3DCurrent%2BTrends%2Band%2BFuture%2BChallenges%2Bof%2BElectrolytes%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.ijhydene.2015.12.090&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b14946&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.ijhydene.2015.12.090&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Current trends and future challenges of electrolytes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Vignarooban, K.; Kushagra, R.; Elango, A.; Badami, P.; Mellander, B.-E.; Xu, X.; Tucker, T. G.; Nam, C.; Kannan, A. M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">International Journal of Hydrogen Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">41</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2829-2846</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">IJHEDX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0360-3199</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Research and development efforts on sodium-ion batteries are gaining momentum due to their potential to accommodate high energy d. coupled with relatively lower cost in comparison with lithium-ion batteries.  In order for the sodium-ion batteries to be com. viable, high performance electrolytes with acceptable ambient temp. ionic cond. and wider electrochem. stability windows are being developed.  A bibliometric anal. of the publications on various types of Na+ ion conducting electrolytes since 1990 shows a total of 200 + publications and reveals an exponential growth in the last few years, due to reasons that the sodium-ion systems promise great potential as the future large scale power sources for variety of applications.  This review consolidates the status of liq. (non-aq., aq. and ionic), polymer gel and solid (ceramics, glasses, and solid polymers) electrolytes and discusses their ionic cond., thermal characteristics, electrochem. stability and viscosity towards applications in sodium-ion batteries.  Among various types available, the non-aq. solvent based electrolyte is the most promising one in terms of ionic cond. even though it is flammable.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpGOUvWFUMgZrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgAWeGB018mcw"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium manganese hexacyanoferrate cathode materials with two structures can be synthesized at room temp.  The sodium-ion concn. in the aq. soln. during pptn. has a strong influence on the compn. and structure of the final product, resulting in different reversible capacity and cycling performance.  With higher Na+ concn., a cooperative Na+ displacement along a single [111] direction induces a transition from cubic to rhombohedral symmetry - this phase transition is reversible on cycling the Na+ concn.  Sodium manganese hexacyanoferrate with an initial rhombohedral structure exhibits a reversible capacity of 134 mA-h/g and retains 120 mA-h/g after 30 cycles.  The high power d. and high rate capability of this material make it a promising cathode material for rechargeable SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqkcibGQmYRi7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lirXuQvRD-1Wg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXosFOltg%253D%253D&amp;md5=bf39a72c1fe501ee77d73e5353bda8a2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201502018"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hierarchical Carbon Framework Wrapped Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as a Superior High-Rate and Extended Lifespan Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A flexible and wearable aq. lithium-ion battery is introduced based on spinel Li1.1Mn2O4 cathode and a carbon-coated NASICON-type LiTi2(PO4)3 anode (NASICON=sodium-ion super ionic conductor).  Energy densities of 63 Wh/kg or 124 mWh/cm3 and power densities of 3 275 W/kg or 11.1 W/cm3 can be obtained, which are seven times larger than the largest reported till now.  The full cell can keep its capacity without significant loss under different bending states, which shows excellent flexibility.  Furthermore, two such flexible cells in series with an operation voltage of 4 V can be compatible with current nonaq. Li-ion batteries.  Therefore, such a flexible cell can potentially be put into practical applications for wearable electronics.  In addn., a self-chargeable unit is realized by integrating a single flexible aq. Li-ion battery with a com. flexible solar cell, which may facilitate the long-time outdoor operation of flexible and wearable electronic devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpg7OwwclsnxbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liclLiCSa50_A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XnsFWltL8%253D&amp;md5=79cb9fe6401804ee4c4e24fa3445769d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1002/ange.201404549"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ren, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">An Ultrastable Anode for Long-Life Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Angew.                         rightTab-cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An electrochemical-thermal coupled overcharge-to-thermal-runaway model for lithium ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ren, Dongsheng; Feng, Xuning; Lu, Languang; Ouyang, Minggao; Zheng, Siqi; Li, Jianqiu; He, Xiangming</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">This paper presents an electrochem.-thermal coupled overcharge-to-thermal-runaway (TR) model to predict the highly interactive electrochem. and thermal behaviors of lithium ion battery under the overcharge conditions.  In this model, the battery voltage equals the difference between the cathode potential and the anode potential, whereas the temp. is predicted by modeling the combined heat generations, including joule heat, thermal runaway reactions and internal short circuit.  The model can fit well with the adiabatic overcharge tests results at 0.33C, 0.5C and 1C, indicating a good capture of the overcharge-to-TR mechanism.  The modeling anal. based on the validated model helps to quantify the heat generation rates of each heat sources during the overcharge-to-TR process.  And the two thermal runaway reactions including the electrolyte oxidn. reaction and the reaction between deposited lithium and electrolyte are found to contribute most to the heat generations during the overcharge process.  Further modeling anal. on the crit. parameters is performed to find possible solns. for the overcharge problem of lithium ion battery.  Increasing the oxidn. potential of the electrolyte, and increasing the onset temp. of thermal runaway are the two effective ways to improve the overcharge performance of lithium ion battery.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGppX4M7sC8_87Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liclLiCSa50_A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtlOrtL3I&amp;md5=7832d2aa8cace2de00aaa8f7e32ebe3b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref11" class="rightTabRefNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit11" data-doi="10.1073/pnas.1314615111"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wong, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We prepd. a phosphazenic compd. triethoxyphosphazen-N-phosphoryldiethylester (PNP) by a facile method and characterized as a flame-retarding electrolyte additive for lithium-ion batteries.  The flammability and electrochem. performance of the PNP-contg. electrolyte were investigated.  The self-extinguishing time value for the electrolyte contg. 10% PNP is decreased 40% compared to base electrolyte, implying strong inhibition of the flammability.  The electrochem. performances of mesocarbon microbeads/Li, LiFePO4/Li and LiMn2O4/Li half-cells in 10% PNP-contg. electrolyte exhibit considerable capacity, coulombic efficiency and cycling stability compared to those for the base electrolyte.                         rightTab-cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Safer Sodium-Ion Battery Based on Nonflammable Organic Phosphate Electrolyte</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zeng Ziqi; Jiang Xiaoyu; Yuan Dingding; Ai Xinping; Yang Hanxi; Cao Yuliang; Li Ran</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are now considered as a low-cost alternative to lithium-ion technologies for large-scale energy storage applications; however, their safety is still a matter of great concern for practical applications.  In this paper, a safer sodium-ion battery is proposed by introducing a nonflammable phosphate electrolyte (trimethyl phosphate, TMP) coupled with NaNi0.35Mn0.35Fe0.3O2 cathode and Sb-based alloy anode.  The physical and electrochemical compatibilities of the TMP electrolyte are investigated by igniting, ionic conductivity, cyclic voltammetry, and charge-discharge measurements.  The results exhibit that the TMP electrolyte with FEC additive is completely nonflammable and has wide electrochemical window (0-4.5 V vs.  Na/Na(+)), in which both the Sb-based anode and NaNi0.35Mn0.35Fe0.3O2 cathode show high reversible capacity and cycling stability, similarly as in carbonate electrolyte.  Based on these results, a nonflammable sodium-ion battery is constructed by use of Sb anode, NaNi0.35Mn0.35Fe0.3O2 cathode, and TMP + 10 vol% FEC electrolyte, which works very well with considerable capacity and cyclability, demonstrating a promising prospect to build safer sodium-ion batteries for large-scale energy storage applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcTKk00v_YolRQqdek-gHSrIfW6udTcc2eb8lQdFbInwALntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC2svnvVWrtg%253D%253D&amp;md5=d85cad700e01cb17d1a849ea41354791</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2007.10.084"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Feng, J. Exptl. results demonstrated that DMMP can solvate considerable amt. of commonly used lithium salts to form non-flammable and Li+-conducting electrolyte, which has very wide electrochem. window (&gt;5 V vs.  Li) and excellent electrochem. compatibility with metallic lithium anode and oxide cathodes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temp. Na-ion batteries (SIBs) showed great promise in grid-scale energy storage, portable electronics, and elec. vehicles because of the abundance of low-cost Na.  Na-based layered oxides with a P2-type layered framework have been considered as one of the most promising cathode materials for SIBs.  However, they suffer from the undesired P2-O2 phase transition, which leads to rapid capacity decay and limited reversible capacities.  Herein, this problem can be significantly mitigated by substituting some of the Ni ions with Mg to obtain Na0.67Mn0.67Ni0.33-xMgxO2 (0â¤xâ¤0.33).  Both the reversible capacity and the capacity retention of the P2-type cathode material were remarkably improved as the P2-O2 phase transition was thus suppressed during cycling.  This strategy might also be applicable to the modulation of the phys. and chem. properties of layered oxides and provides new insight into the rational design of high-capacity and highly stable cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqs1w8P4jHWorVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg4B5MKY31fIQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XntFCmtLo%253D&amp;md5=db98750372bffe253f4c4d51e3f452cf</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref31" class="rightTabRefNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit31" data-doi="10.1039/C6CC09378G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, P.-F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yao, H.-R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zuo, T.-T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yin, Y.-X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, Y.-G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Novel P2-Type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Mg<sub>1/6</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as a promising alternative for the next generation of electric storage systems due to their similar electrochemistry to Li-ion batteries and the low cost of sodium resources.  Exploring appropriate electrode materials with decent electrochemical performance is the key issue for development of sodium ion batteries.  Due to the high structural stability, facile reaction mechanism and rich structural diversity, phosphate framework materials have attracted increasing attention as promising electrode materials for sodium ion batteries.  Herein, we review the latest advances and progresses in the exploration of phosphate framework materials especially related to single-phosphates, pyrophosphates and mixed-phosphates.  We provide the detailed and comprehensive understanding of structure-composition-performance relationship of materials and try to show the advantages and disadvantages of the materials for use in SIBs.  In addition, some new perspectives about phosphate framework materials for SIBs are also discussed.  Phosphate framework materials will be a competitive and attractive choice for use as electrodes in the next-generation of energy storage devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcTMcbcHlPuAMBwLZxei1HN8fW6udTcc2eZqF3aCJslFX7ntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1cnhsFehsQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=5afdb3cd74a61df70f70c83d0ead6440</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref36" class="rightTabRefNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit36" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b12620"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wei, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Highly Crystallized Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries offer an attractive option for low cost grid scale storage due to the abundance of Na.  Sn is touted as a high capacity anode for Na-ion batteries with a high theor. capacity of 847 mA-h/g, but it has several limitations such as large vol. expansion with cycling, slow kinetics, and unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation.  An anode consisting of a Sn thin film deposited on a hierarchical wood fiber substrate simultaneously addresses all the challenges assocd. with Sn anodes.  The soft nature of wood fibers effectively releases the mech. stresses assocd. with the sodiation process, and the mesoporous structure functions as an electrolyte reservoir that allows for ion transport through the outer and inner surface of the fiber. A stable cycling performance of 400 cycles with an initial capacity of 339 mA-h/g is demonstrated; a significant improvement over other reported Sn nanostructures. The good Na-ion insertion property is attributed to the short diffusion distance in the hollow carbon nanowires and the large interlayer distance (0.37 nm) between the graphitic sheets, which agrees with the interlayered distance predicted by theor. calcns. to enable Na-ion insertion in carbon materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq9utH1EdajJbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhr9DV9f90OJA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XotlShu78%253D&amp;md5=c54fcb809a340c023d9185f1d9f5e848</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref43" class="rightTabRefNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit43" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2016.02.164"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jiang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanospherical-Like Manganese Monoxide/Reduced Graphene Oxide Composite Synthesized by Electron Beam Radiation as Anode Material for High-Performance Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Electrochim. These results suggest promising applications of the NVP@rGO material as a high performance cathode for Na ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrH7vTFba3_x7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhr9DV9f90OJA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXisVWisr4%253D&amp;md5=730aad8d8279319811c287ad25fae0c1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref51" class="rightTabRefNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit51" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201502197"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium Ion Batteries: 3d Graphene Decorated NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> Microspheres as a Superior High-Rate and Ultracycle-Stable Anode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         rightTab-cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In search of an optimized electrolyte for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ponrouch, Alexandre; Marchante, Elena; Courty, Matthieu; Tarascon, Jean-Marie; Palacin, M. Rosa</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrolytes are essential for the proper functioning of any battery technol. and the emerging Na-ion technol. is no exception.  Hence, a major focus on battery research is to identify the most appropriate formulation so as to minimize interface reactions and enhance both cell performances and safety aspects.  In order to identify suitable electrolyte formulations for Na-ion chem. we benchmarked various electrolytes contg. diverse solvent mixts. (cyclic, acyclic carbonates, glymes) and Na-based salts having either F-based or perchlorate anions and measured viscosity, ionic cond., and thermal and electrochem. stability.  The binary EC:PC solvent mixt. has emerged as the best solvent formulation and has been used to test the performance of Na/hard carbon cells with both NaClO4 and NaPF6 as dissolved salts.  Hard carbon electrodes having reversible capacities of 200 mA h g-1 with decent rate capability and excellent capacity retention (&gt;180 cycles) were demonstrated.  Moreover, DSC heating curves demonstrated that fully sodiated hard carbon cycled in NaPF6-EC:PC exhibits the highest exothermic onset temp. and nearly the lowest enthalpy of reaction, thus making this electrolyte most attractive for the development of Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp6iY9--X3CQLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liorlRgqQxilw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38Xht1WrsLnE&amp;md5=4cc627e9703d3883d10ee8eccc42e514</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref53" class="rightTabRefNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit53" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA04491J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Porous NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> Nanocubes: A High-Rate Nonaqueous Sodium Anode Material with More Than 10 000 Cycle Life</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Besides targeting low-voltage intercalation, NaTi2(PO4)3 (NTP) is a promising neg. electrode material for non-aq. sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  However, the low electronic cond. of this material inhibits its potential applications.  Herein, we report the controllable synthesis of four interesting porous NTP nanocubes via a one-pot solvothermal method.  The as-synthesized products have shown excellent high-rate performance and cycling stability as SIB anodes.  After 10 000 cycles at a 10C rate, 75.5% of the initial capacity is retained, which exceeds those of most of the reported SIB anode materials.  Even at an extremely high rate of 100C, the NTP nanocubes can still deliver considerable reversible capacities after deep charging/discharging for 15 000 cycles.  The superior electrochem. performances can be attributed to their unique nanostructures.  We hope that such a finding of the new construction will enrich the NTP system and provide several possible candidates for SIB anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqcwgKt1_heH7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liorlRgqQxilw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht1yqtbnN&amp;md5=7bb066b2f28f142e2bb451a501b20a7e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref54" class="rightTabRefNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit54" data-doi="10.1007/s12274-016-1077-y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Roh, H.-K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, H.-K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, M.-S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, D.-H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chung, K.                         rightTab-cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ synthesis of chemically bonded NaTi2(PO4)3/rGO 2D nanocomposite for high-rate sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Roh, Ha-Kyung; Kim, Hyun-Kyung; Kim, Myeong-Seong; Kim, Dong-Hyun; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Roh, Kwang Chul; Kim, Kwang-Bum</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Research</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A phase-pure NaTi2(PO4)3/reduced graphene oxide (rGO) nanocomposite was prepd. using a microwave-assisted one-pot method and subsequent heat treatment. The chem. interactions between the NaTi2(PO4)3 nanoparticles and rGO could immobilize the NaTi2(PO4)3 nanoparticles on the rGO sheets, which might be responsible for the excellent electrochem. performance of the nanocomposite.  The NaTi2(PO4)3/rGO nanocomposite exhibited a specific capacity of 128.6 mAÂ·hÂ·g-1 approaching the theor. value at a 0.1 C-rate with an excellent rate capability (72.9% capacity retention at 50 C-rate) and cycling performance (only 4.5% capacity loss after 1,000 cycles at a high rate of 10 C).  These properties were maintained even when the electrodes were prepd. without the use of an addnl. conducting agent.  The excellent sodium storage properties of the NaTi2(PO4)3/rGO nanocomposite could be attributed to the nano-sized NaTi2(PO4)3 particles, which significantly reduced the transport lengths for Na+ ions, and an intimate contact between the NaTi2(PO4)3 particles and rGO due to chem. bonding.                         rightTab-cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Current trends and future challenges of electrolytes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Vignarooban, K.; Kushagra, R.; Elango, A.; Badami, P.; Mellander, B.-E.; Xu, X.; Tucker, T. G.; Nam, C.; Kannan, A. M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">International Journal of Hydrogen Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">41</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2829-2846</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">IJHEDX</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Research and development efforts on sodium-ion batteries are gaining momentum due to their potential to accommodate high energy d. coupled with relatively lower cost in comparison with lithium-ion batteries.  In order for the sodium-ion batteries to be com. viable, high performance electrolytes with acceptable ambient temp. ionic cond. and wider electrochem. stability windows are being developed.  A bibliometric anal. of the publications on various types of Na+ ion conducting electrolytes since 1990 shows a total of 200 + publications and reveals an exponential growth in the last few years, due to reasons that the sodium-ion systems promise great potential as the future large scale power sources for variety of applications.  This review consolidates the status of liq. (non-aq., aq. and ionic), polymer gel and solid (ceramics, glasses, and solid polymers) electrolytes and discusses their ionic cond., thermal characteristics, electrochem. stability and viscosity towards applications in sodium-ion batteries.  Among various types available, the non-aq. solvent based electrolyte is the most promising one in terms of ionic cond. even though it is flammable.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpGOUvWFUMgZrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liorlRgqQxilw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtF2qtA%253D%253D&amp;md5=5d8ac8500e4ce3dfa33e62fa4a4fcf4b</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li></ul></div></div></div></div></article></main>

(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

<script nonce="90f6e9c81d48775c-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e9c81d48775c-SJC">
"use strict"
//Script checks gdpr cookie and submits form
	/* 
	Create an in-page pop-up message, only for the InfoCentral homepage, that will gather email registrants for the library marketing list.
	 
	Needs the end point
	*/

var infoCentralCapture = (function() {

    //Detect mobile device
    window.mobilecheck = function() {
        var check = false;
        (function(a){if(/(android|bb\d+|meego).+mobile|avantgo|bada\/|blackberry|blazer|compal|elaine|fennec|hiptop|iemobile|ip(hone|od)|iris|kindle|lge |maemo|midp|mmp|mobile.+firefox|netfront|opera u|g560|gene|gf\-5|g\-mo|go(\.w|od)|gr(ad|un)|haie|hcit|hd\-(m|p|t)|hei\-|hi(pt|ta)|hp( i|ip)|hs\-c|ht(c(\-| |_|a|g|p|s|t)|tp)|hu(aw|tc)|i\-(20|go|ma)|i230|iac( |\-|\/)|ibro|idea|ig01|ikom|im1k|inno|ipaq|iris|ja(t|v)a|jbro|jemu|jigs|kddi|keji|kgt( |\/)|klon|kpt |kwc\-|kyo(c|k)|le(no|xi)|lg( g|\/(k|l|u)|50|54|\-[a-w])|libw|lynx|m1\-w|m3ga|m50\/|ma(te|ui|xo)|mc(01|21|ca)|m\-cr|me(rc|ri)|mi(o8|oa|ts)|mmef|mo(01|02|bi|de|do|t(\-| |o|v)|zz)|mt(50|p1|v )|mwbp|mywa|n10[0-2]|n20[2-3]|n30(0|2)|n50(0|2|5)|n7(0(0|1)|10)|ne((c|m)\-|on|tf|wf|wg|wt)|nok(6|i)|nzph|o2im|op(ti|wv)|oran|owg1|p800|pan(a|d|t)|pdxg|pg(13|\-([1-8]|c))|phil|pire|pl(ay|uc)|pn\-2|po(ck|rt|se)|prox|psio|pt\-g|qa\-a|qc(07|12|21|32|60|\-[2-7]|i\-)|qtek|r380|r600|raks|rim9|ro(ve|zo)|s55\/|sa(ge|ma|mm|ms|ny|va)|sc(01|h\-|oo|p\-)|sdk\/|se(c(\-|0|1)|47|mc|nd|ri)|sgh\-|shar|sie(\-|m)|sk\-0|sl(45|id)|sm(al|ar|b3|it|t5)|so(ft|ny)|sp(01|h\-|v\-|v )|sy(01|mb)|t2(18|50)|t6(00|10|18)|ta(gt|lk)|tcl\-|tdg\-|tel(i|m)|tim\-|t\-mo|to(pl|sh)|ts(70|m\-|m3|m5)|tx\-9|up(\.b|g1|si)|utst|v400|v750|veri|vi(rg|te)|vk(40|5[0-3]|\-v)|vm40|voda|vulc|vx(52|53|60|61|70|80|81|83|85|98)|w3c(\-| )|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>
